<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 27 languages"   >

	</label>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a96ffdebfe1e2455ad8274199662d0e4fd0c3b21" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.177ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (\{3,5\},\{6\})\}}"></span> </figcaption></figure>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>hypergraph</b> is a generalization of a <a href="/wiki/Graph_(discrete_mathematics)" title="Graph (discrete mathematics)">graph</a> in which an <a href="/wiki/Graph_theory" title="Graph theory">edge</a> can join any number of <a href="/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)" title="Vertex (graph theory)">vertices</a>. In contrast, in an ordinary graph, an edge connects exactly two vertices.
</p><p>Formally, a <b>directed hypergraph</b> is a pair <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle (X,E)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (X,E)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/09abc2916dda67f75618cdfa4d8c79c631ed013e" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.599ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (X,E)}"></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span> is a set of elements called <i>nodes</i>, <i>vertices</i>, <i>points</i>, or <i>elements</i> and <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle E}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>E</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle E}"></span> is a set of pairs of subsets of <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span>. Each of these pairs <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle (D,C)\in E}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>D</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>C</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mi>E</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (D,C)\in E}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c074906f49a4a3a38b843056fdb86e4fc42d264" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.15ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (D,C)\in E}"></span> is called an <i>edge</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Hyperedge" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyperedge">hyperedge</a></i>;  the vertex subset <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle D}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>D</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle D}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle D}"></span> is known as its <i>tail</i> or <i>domain</i>, and  <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle C}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>C</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4fc55753007cd3c18576f7933f6f089196732029" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.766ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle C}"></span> as its  <i>head</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Codomain" title="Codomain">codomain</a></i>.
</p><p>The <b>order of a hypergraph</b> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle (X,E)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (X,E)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/09abc2916dda67f75618cdfa4d8c79c631ed013e" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.599ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (X,E)}"></span> is the number of vertices in <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/68baa052181f707c662844a465bfeeb135e82bab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.98ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle X}"></span>. The <b>size of the hypergraph</b> is the number of edges in <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle E}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>E</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle E}"></span>. The <b>order of an edge</b> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle e=(D,C)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>e</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>D</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>C</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle e=(D,C)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/02d9a9f2b93162a637836f9da8c3692889c2e7ac" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.716ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle e=(D,C)}"></span> in a directed hypergraph is <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle |e|=(|D|,|C|)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>e</mi>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>D</mi>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>C</mi>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |e|=(|D|,|C|)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/787ac3364bfc521ab6400b6faec8090a607bbb3a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.597ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle |e|=(|D|,|C|)}"></span>: that is, the number of vertices in its tail followed by the number of vertices in its head.
</p><p>The definition above generalizes from a <a href="/wiki/Directed_graph" title="Directed graph">directed graph</a> to a directed hypergraph by defining the head or tail of each edge as a set of vertices (<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle C\subseteq X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>C</mi>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C\subseteq X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a98beab5a3637ffa2321ef617c02a3b5b26ffd3a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:6.845ex; height:2.343ex;" alt="{\displaystyle C\subseteq X}"></span> or <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle D\subseteq X}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>D</mi>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle D\subseteq X}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/41d3949fca38567b9801a052056fbba32fd0ea11" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:7.003ex; height:2.343ex;" alt="{\displaystyle D\subseteq X}"></span>) rather than as a single vertex. A graph is then the special case where each of these sets contains only one element. Hence any standard graph theoretic concept that is independent of the edge orders <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle |e|}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>e</mi>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |e|}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/50511419e12af8b5bbc64a26209d8467fe8862d7" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:2.377ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle |e|}"></span> will generalize to hypergraph theory.
</p><p>An <b>undirected hypergraph</b> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle (X,E)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (X,E)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/09abc2916dda67f75618cdfa4d8c79c631ed013e" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.599ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle (X,E)}"></span> is an undirected graph whose edges connect not just two vertices, but an arbitrary number.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> An undirected hypergraph is also called a <i>set system</i> or a <i><a href="/wiki/Family_of_sets" title="Family of sets">family of sets</a></i> drawn from the <a href="/wiki/Universal_set" title="Universal set">universal set</a>.
</p><p>Hypergraphs can be viewed as <a href="/wiki/Incidence_structure" title="Incidence structure">incidence structures</a>.  In particular, there is a bipartite "incidence graph" or "<a href="/wiki/Levi_graph" title="Levi graph">Levi graph</a>" corresponding to every hypergraph, and conversely, every <a href="/wiki/Bipartite_graph" title="Bipartite graph">bipartite graph</a> can be regarded as the incidence graph of a hypergraph when it is 2-colored and it is indicated which color class corresponds to hypergraph vertices and which to hypergraph edges.
</p><p>Hypergraphs have many other names.  In <a href="/wiki/Computational_geometry" title="Computational geometry">computational geometry</a>, an undirected hypergraph may sometimes be called a <b>range space</b> and then the hyperedges are called <i>ranges</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
In <a href="/wiki/Cooperative_game_theory" title="Cooperative game theory">cooperative game</a> theory, hypergraphs are called <b>simple games</b> (voting games); this notion is applied to solve problems in <a href="/wiki/Social_choice_theory" title="Social choice theory">social choice theory</a>.  In some literature edges are referred to as <i>hyperlinks</i> or <i>connectors</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The collection of hypergraphs is a <a href="/wiki/Category_(mathematics)" title="Category (mathematics)">category</a> with hypergraph <a href="/wiki/Homomorphism" title="Homomorphism">homomorphisms</a> as <a href="/wiki/Morphism" title="Morphism">morphisms</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc">
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Undirected hypergraphs are useful in modelling such things as satisfiability problems,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> databases,<sup id="cite_ref-database_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-database-6"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> machine learning,<sup id="cite_ref-hyperx_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hyperx-7"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Steiner_tree_problem" title="Steiner tree problem">Steiner tree problems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> They have been extensively used in <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> tasks as the data model and classifier <a href="/wiki/Regularization_(mathematics)" title="Regularization (mathematics)">regularization (mathematics)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The applications include <a href="/wiki/Recommender_system" title="Recommender system">recommender system</a> (communities as hyperedges),<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Image_retrieval" title="Image retrieval">image retrieval</a> (correlations as hyperedges),<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a> (biochemical interactions as hyperedges).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Representative hypergraph learning techniques include hypergraph <a href="/wiki/Spectral_clustering" title="Spectral clustering">spectral clustering</a> that extends the <a href="/wiki/Spectral_graph_theory" title="Spectral graph theory">spectral graph theory</a> with hypergraph Laplacian,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and hypergraph <a href="/wiki/Semi-supervised_learning" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-supervised learning">semi-supervised learning</a> that introduces extra hypergraph structural cost to restrict the learning results.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> For large scale hypergraphs, a distributed framework<sup id="cite_ref-hyperx_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hyperx-7"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> built using <a href="/wiki/Apache_Spark" title="Apache Spark">Apache Spark</a> is also available. It can be desirable to study hypergraphs where all hyperedges have the same cardinality; a <i>k-uniform hypergraph</i> is a hypergraph such that all its hyperedges have size <i>k</i>. (In other words, one such hypergraph is a collection of sets, each such set a hyperedge connecting <i>k</i> nodes.) So a 2-uniform hypergraph is a graph, a 3-uniform hypergraph is a collection of unordered triples, and so on.
</p><p>Directed hypergraphs can be used to model things including telephony applications,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> detecting <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> operations research,<sup id="cite_ref-ausiello_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ausiello-18"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and transportation planning. They can also be used to model <a href="/wiki/Horn-satisfiability" title="Horn-satisfiability">Horn-satisfiability</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gallo_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gallo-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Generalizations_of_concepts_from_graphs">Generalizations of concepts from graphs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Generalizations of concepts from graphs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorems</a> and concepts involving graphs also hold for hypergraphs, in particular:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Matching_in_hypergraphs" title="Matching in hypergraphs">Matching in hypergraphs</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vertex_cover_in_hypergraphs" title="Vertex cover in hypergraphs">Vertex cover in hypergraphs</a> (also known as: <b>transversal</b>);</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Line_graph_of_a_hypergraph" title="Line graph of a hypergraph">Line graph of a hypergraph</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hypergraph_grammar" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypergraph grammar">Hypergraph grammar</a> - created by augmenting a class of hypergraphs with a set of replacement rules;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ramsey%27s_theorem" title="Ramsey's theorem">Ramsey's theorem</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Erd%C5%91s%E2%80%93Ko%E2%80%93Rado_theorem" title="ErdsKoRado theorem">ErdsKoRado theorem</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kruskal%E2%80%93Katona_theorem" title="KruskalKatona theorem">KruskalKatona theorem</a> on uniform hypergraphs;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hall-type_theorems_for_hypergraphs" title="Hall-type theorems for hypergraphs">Hall-type theorems for hypergraphs</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In directed hypergraphs: <a href="/wiki/Transitive_closure" title="Transitive closure">transitive closure</a>, and shortest path problems.<sup id="cite_ref-ausiello_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ausiello-18"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Hypergraph_drawing">Hypergraph drawing</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hypergraph drawing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CircuitoDosMallas.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/CircuitoDosMallas.png/250px-CircuitoDosMallas.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/CircuitoDosMallas.png 1.5x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="225"></a><figcaption>This <a href="/wiki/Circuit_diagram" title="Circuit diagram">circuit diagram</a> can be interpreted as a drawing of a hypergraph in which four vertices (depicted as white rectangles and disks) are connected by three hyperedges drawn as trees.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although hypergraphs are more difficult to draw on paper than graphs, several researchers have studied methods for the visualization of hypergraphs.
</p><p>In one possible visual representation for hypergraphs, similar to the standard <a href="/wiki/Graph_drawing" title="Graph drawing">graph drawing</a> style in which curves in the plane are used to depict graph edges, a hypergraph's vertices are depicted as points, disks, or boxes, and its hyperedges are depicted as trees that have the vertices as their leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> If the vertices are represented as points, the hyperedges may also be shown as smooth curves that connect sets of points, or as <a href="/wiki/Simple_closed_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Simple closed curve">simple closed curves</a> that enclose sets of points.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg/250px-Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="207" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg/375px-Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg/500px-Venn%27s_four_ellipse_construction.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="390"></a><figcaption>An order-4 Venn diagram, which can be interpreted as a subdivision drawing of a hypergraph with 15 vertices (the 15 colored regions) and 4 hyperedges (the 4 ellipses)</figcaption></figure>
<p>In another style of hypergraph visualization, the subdivision model of hypergraph drawing,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> the plane is subdivided into regions, each of which represents a single vertex of the hypergraph. The hyperedges of the hypergraph are represented by contiguous subsets of these regions, which may be indicated by coloring, by drawing outlines around them, or both. An order-<i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagram</a>, for instance, may be viewed as a subdivision drawing of a hypergraph with <i>n</i> hyperedges (the curves defining the diagram) and 2<sup><i>n</i></sup>&nbsp;&nbsp;1 vertices (represented by the regions into which these curves subdivide the plane). In contrast with the polynomial-time recognition of <a href="/wiki/Planar_graph" title="Planar graph">planar graphs</a>, it is <a href="/wiki/NP-complete" class="mw-redirect" title="NP-complete">NP-complete</a> to determine whether a hypergraph has a planar subdivision drawing,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> but the existence of a drawing of this type may be tested efficiently when the adjacency pattern of the regions is constrained to be a path, cycle, or tree.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative representation of the hypergraph called PAOH<sup id="cite_ref-paoh_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paoh-1"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> is shown in the figure on top of this article. Edges are vertical lines connecting vertices. Vertices are aligned on the left. The legend on the right shows the names of the edges. It has been designed for dynamic hypergraphs but can be used for simple hypergraphs as well.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Hypergraph_coloring">Hypergraph coloring</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hypergraph coloring"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Classic hypergraph coloring is assigning one of the colors from set <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \{1,2,3,...,\lambda \}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">{</mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>2</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mn>3</mn>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi><!--  --></mi>
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">}</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \{1,2,3,...,\lambda \}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c63c87711c0ed94e5977b1586fd5e01a6161cab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:14.405ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \{1,2,3,...,\lambda \}}"></span> to every vertex of a hypergraph in such a way that each hyperedge contains at least two vertices of distinct colors. In other words, there must be no monochromatic hyperedge with cardinality at least 2. In this sense it is a direct generalization of graph coloring. The minimum number of used distinct colors over all colorings is called the chromatic number of a hypergraph.
</p><p>Hypergraphs for which there exists a coloring using up to <i>k</i> colors are referred to as <i>k-colorable</i>. The 2-colorable hypergraphs are exactly the bipartite ones.
</p><p>There are many generalizations of classic hypergraph coloring. One of them is the so-called mixed hypergraph coloring, when monochromatic edges are allowed. Some mixed hypergraphs are uncolorable for any number of colors. A general criterion for uncolorability is unknown.  When a mixed hypergraph is colorable, then the minimum and maximum number of used colors are called the lower and upper chromatic numbers respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Properties_of_hypergraphs">Properties of hypergraphs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Properties of hypergraphs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A hypergraph can have various properties, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Empty</b> - has no edges.</li>
<li><b>Non-simple</b> <i>(or</i> <b>multiple</b><i>)</i> - has loops (hyperedges with a single vertex) or repeated edges, which means there can be two or more edges containing the same set of vertices.</li>
<li><b>Simple</b> - has no loops and no repeated edges.</li>
<li><b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle d}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>d</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle d}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.216ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle d}"></span>-regular</b> - every vertex has degree <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle d}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>d</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle d}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.216ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle d}"></span>, i.e., contained in exactly <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle d}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>d</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle d}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.216ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle d}"></span> hyperedges.</li>
<li><b>2-colorable</b> - its vertices can be partitioned into two classes <i>U</i> and <i>V</i> in such a way that each hyperedge with cardinality at least 2 contains at least one vertex from both classes. An alternative term is <b><a href="/wiki/Property_B" title="Property B">Property B</a></b>.
<ul><li>Two stronger properties are <a href="/wiki/Bipartite_hypergraph" title="Bipartite hypergraph"><b>bipartite</b></a> and <a href="/wiki/Balanced_hypergraph" title="Balanced hypergraph"><b>balanced</b></a>.</li></ul></li>
<li><b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span>-uniform</b> - each hyperedge contains precisely <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span> vertices.</li>
<li><b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span>-partite</b> - the vertices are partitioned into <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span> parts, and each hyperedge contains precisely one vertex of each type.
<ul><li>Every <b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span>-partite</b> hypergraph (for  <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k\geq 2}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mn>2</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k\geq 2}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c797a67c0a51167d373c013a9a020f4568a11754" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:5.472ex; height:2.343ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k\geq 2}"></span>) is both  <b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>k</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span></b>-uniform and bipartite (and 2-colorable).</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Reduced</b>:<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> no hyperedge is a strict subset of another hyperedge; equivalently, every hyperedge is maximal for inclusion. The <b>reduction</b> of a hypergraph is the reduced hypergraph obtained by removing every hyperedge which is included in another hyperedge.</li>
<li><b>Downward-closed</b> - every subset of an undirected hypergraph's edges is a hyperedge too. A downward-closed hypergraph is usually called an <b><a href="/wiki/Abstract_simplicial_complex" title="Abstract simplicial complex">abstract simplicial complex</a></b>. It is generally not reduced, unless all hyperedges have cardinality 1.
<ul><li>An abstract simplicial complex with the <i>augmentation property</i> is called a <b><a href="/wiki/Matroid" title="Matroid">matroid</a></b>.</li></ul></li>
<li><b>Laminar</b>: for any two hyperedges, either they are disjoint, or one is included in the other. In other words, the set of hyperedges forms a <a href="/wiki/Laminar_set_family" title="Laminar set family">laminar set family</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Related_hypergraphs">Related hypergraphs</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Related hypergraphs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Because hypergraph links can have any cardinality, there are several notions of the concept of a subgraph, called <i>subhypergraphs</i>, <i>partial hypergraphs</i> and <i>section hypergraphs</i>.
</p><p>Let <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H=(X,E)}">
  <semantics>

        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H=(X,E)}</annotation>

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle X=\lbrace x_{i}\mid i\in I_{v}\rbrace ,}">

<p>and having <i>edge set</i>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle E=\lbrace e_{i}\mid i\in I_{e},e_{i}\subseteq X,e_{i}\neq \emptyset \rbrace ,}">

<p>When a notion of equality is properly defined, as done below, the operation of taking the dual of a hypergraph is an <a href="/wiki/Involution_(mathematics)" title="Involution (mathematics)">involution</a>, i.e.,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H.}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msup>
          <mrow>
            <mo>(</mo>
            <msup>
              <mi>H</mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mo><!--  --></mo>
              </mrow>
            </msup>
            <mo>)</mo>
          </mrow>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mo><!--  --></mo>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f6c701e2faec0d8b33bf4039b429db34910a4921" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.83ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H.}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Connected_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Connected graph">connected graph</a> <i>G</i> with the same vertex set as a connected hypergraph <i>H</i> is a <b>host graph</b> for <i>H</i> if every hyperedge of <i>H</i> <a href="/wiki/Induced_subgraph" title="Induced subgraph">induces</a> a connected subgraph in  <i>G</i>. For a disconnected hypergraph <i>H</i>, <i>G</i> is a host graph if there is a bijection between the <a href="/wiki/Connected_component_(graph_theory)" class="mw-redirect" title="Connected component (graph theory)">connected components</a> of <i>G</i> and of <i>H</i>, such that each connected component <i>G'</i> of <i>G</i> is a host of the corresponding <i>H'</i>.
</p><p>The <b>2-section</b> (or <b>clique graph</b>, <b>representing graph</b>, <b>primal graph</b>, <b>Gaifman graph</b>) of a hypergraph is the graph with the same vertices of the hypergraph, and edges between all pairs of vertices contained in the same hyperedge.
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Incidence_graph">Incidence graph</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Incidence graph"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A hypergraph <i>H</i> may be represented by a <a href="/wiki/Bipartite_graph" title="Bipartite graph">bipartite graph</a> <i>BG</i> as follows: the sets <i>X</i> and <i>E</i> are the parts of <i>BG</i>, and (<i>x<sub>1</sub></i>,  <i>e<sub>1</sub></i>) are connected with an edge if and only if vertex <i>x<sub>1</sub></i> is contained in edge <i>e<sub>1</sub></i> in <i>H</i>.
</p><p>Conversely, any bipartite graph with fixed parts and no unconnected nodes in the second part represents some hypergraph in the manner described above. This bipartite graph is also called <a href="/wiki/Incidence_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Incidence graph">incidence graph</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Adjacency_matrix">Adjacency matrix</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Adjacency matrix"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A parallel for the adjacency matrix of a hypergraph can be drawn from the <a href="/wiki/Adjacency_matrix" title="Adjacency matrix">adjacency matrix</a> of a graph. In the case of a graph, the adjacency matrix is a square matrix which indicates whether pairs of vertices are <a href="/wiki/Neighbourhood_(graph_theory)" title="Neighbourhood (graph theory)">adjacent</a>. Likewise, we can define the adjacency matrix <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle A=(a_{ij})}">

            <mi>j</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cycles">Cycles</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cycles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In contrast with ordinary undirected graphs for which there is a single natural notion of <a href="/wiki/Cycle_(graph_theory)" title="Cycle (graph theory)">cycles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forest_(graph_theory)" class="mw-redirect" title="Forest (graph theory)">acyclic graphs</a>. For hypergraphs, there are multiple natural non-equivalent definitions of cycles which collapse to the ordinary notion of cycle when the graph case is considered.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Berge_cycles">Berge cycles</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Berge cycles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A first notion of cycle was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Berge" title="Claude Berge">Claude Berge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A <b>Berge cycle</b> in a hypergraph is an alternating sequence of distinct vertices and edges <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle (v_{1},e_{1},\dots ,v_{n},e_{n})}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>1</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>e</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>1</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>e</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle (v_{1},e_{1},\dots ,v_{n},e_{n})}</annotation>
  </semantics>

</p><p>Under this definition a hypergraph is <b>acyclic</b> if and only if its <a href="/wiki/Incidence_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Incidence graph">incidence graph</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Bipartite_graph" title="Bipartite graph">bipartite graph</a> defined above) is acyclic. Thus Berge-cyclicity can obviously be tested in <a href="/wiki/Linear_time" class="mw-redirect" title="Linear time">linear time</a> by an exploration of the incidence graph.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tight_cycles">Tight cycles</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tight cycles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

</p><p>Rdl, Szemerdi, and Ruciski showed that every <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle n}">

This corresponds to an approximate hypergraph-extension of the celebrated <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_path#BondyChvtal_theorem" title="Hamiltonian path"> Dirac's theorem</a> about Hamilton cycles in graphs.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The maximum number of hyperedges in a (tightly) acyclic <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span>-uniform hypergraph remains unknown. The best known bounds, obtained by Sudakov and Tomon,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> show that every <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle n}">

      </mstyle>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n}"></span>-vertex <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle k}">
  <semantics>

      </mstyle>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.211ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle k}"></span>-uniform hypergraph with at least <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle n^{k-1+o(1)}}">

      </mstyle>

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n^{k-1+o(1)}}</annotation>
  </semantics>

This bound is optimal up to the <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle o(1)}">

        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle o(1)}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d77278222a965f6f092279969a224935d9917916" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.099ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle o(1)}"></span> error term.
</p><p>An <b><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \ell }">

It consists in a sequence of vertices <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle v_{1},\dots ,v_{n}}">

</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="-acyclicity"><span id=".CE.B1-acyclicity"></span>-acyclicity</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: -acyclicity"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The definition of Berge-acyclicity might seem to be very restrictive: for instance, if a hypergraph has some pair <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle v\neq v'}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>v</mi>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mo></mo>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v\neq v'}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9e9176479d4c75d442184a84395b209587d861a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.038ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle v\neq v'}"></span> of vertices and some pair <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle f\neq f'}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>f</mi>
          <mo></mo>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f\neq f'}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5a92c1583b35c8a81347d85196079d78fbfd18c5" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.382ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle f\neq f'}"></span> of hyperedges such that <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle v,v'\in f}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>v</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mo></mo>
        </msup>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v,v'\in f}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d53e96317e592306a6158fc2682b598744bec0fb" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.093ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle v,v'\in f}"></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle v,v'\in f'}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>v</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mo></mo>
        </msup>
        <mo><!--  --></mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>f</mi>
          <mo></mo>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v,v'\in f'}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f2d98c628b2c0c534d1565a663532ba0041d6c2b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.82ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle v,v'\in f'}"></span>, then it is Berge-cyclic. 
</p><p>We can define a weaker notion of hypergraph acyclicity,<sup id="cite_ref-database_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-database-6"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> later termed -acyclicity. This notion of acyclicity is equivalent to the hypergraph being conformal (every clique of the primal graph is covered by some hyperedge) and its primal graph being <a href="/wiki/Chordal_graph" title="Chordal graph">chordal</a>; it is also equivalent to reducibility to the empty graph through the <a href="/wiki/GYO_algorithm" title="GYO algorithm">GYO algorithm</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-graham1979universal_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham1979universal-38"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> (also known as Graham's algorithm), a <a href="/wiki/Confluence_(abstract_rewriting)" title="Confluence (abstract rewriting)">confluent</a> iterative process which removes hyperedges using a generalized definition of <a href="/wiki/Ear_(graph_theory)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ear (graph theory)">ears</a>. In the domain of <a href="/wiki/Database_theory" title="Database theory">database theory</a>, it is known that a <a href="/wiki/Database_schema" title="Database schema">database schema</a> enjoys certain desirable properties if its underlying hypergraph is -acyclic.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Besides, -acyclicity is also related to the expressiveness of the <a href="/wiki/Guarded_fragment" class="mw-redirect" title="Guarded fragment">guarded fragment</a> of <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">first-order logic</a>.
</p><p>We can test in <a href="/wiki/Linear_time" class="mw-redirect" title="Linear time">linear time</a> if a hypergraph is -acyclic.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Note that -acyclicity has the counter-intuitive property that adding hyperedges to an -cyclic hypergraph may make it -acyclic (for instance, adding a hyperedge containing all vertices of the hypergraph will always make it -acyclic). Motivated in part by this perceived shortcoming, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fagin" title="Ronald Fagin">Ronald Fagin</a><sup id="cite_ref-fagin1983degrees_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fagin1983degrees-41"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> defined the stronger notions of -acyclicity and -acyclicity. We can state -acyclicity as the requirement that all subhypergraphs of the hypergraph are -acyclic, which is equivalent<sup id="cite_ref-fagin1983degrees_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fagin1983degrees-41"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> to an earlier definition by Graham.<sup id="cite_ref-graham1979universal_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham1979universal-38"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The notion of -acyclicity is a more restrictive condition which is equivalent to several desirable properties of database schemas and is related to <a href="/wiki/Bachman_diagram" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachman diagram">Bachman diagrams</a>. Both -acyclicity and -acyclicity can be tested in <a href="/wiki/PTIME" class="mw-redirect" title="PTIME">polynomial time</a>.
</p><p>Those four notions of acyclicity are comparable: -acyclicity which implies -acyclicity which implies -acyclicity. Moreover, Berge-acyclicity implies all of them. None of the reverse implications hold including the Berge one. In other words, these four notions are different.<sup id="cite_ref-fagin1983degrees_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fagin1983degrees-41"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Isomorphism,_symmetry,_and_equality"><span id="Isomorphism.2C_symmetry.2C_and_equality"></span>Isomorphism, symmetry, and equality</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Isomorphism, symmetry, and equality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A hypergraph <a href="/wiki/Homomorphism" title="Homomorphism">homomorphism</a> is a map from the vertex set of one hypergraph to another such that each edge maps to one other edge.
</p><p>A hypergraph <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H=(X,E)}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>H</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>X</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>

<p>The bijection <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \phi }">

Note that

<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H\simeq G}">

<p>When the edges of a hypergraph are explicitly labeled, one has the additional notion of <i>strong isomorphism</i>. One says that <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>H</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H}"></span> is <i>strongly isomorphic</i> to <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle G}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>G</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5f3c8921a3b352de45446a6789b104458c9f90b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.827ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle G}"></span> if the permutation is the identity. One then writes <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H\cong G}">

      </mstyle>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ad1502c87b67de111ef1ccfe07ec7857b2898210" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.989ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H\cong G}"></span>.  Note that all strongly isomorphic graphs are isomorphic, but not vice versa.

<p>Note that
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H\equiv G}">

<p>If, in addition, the permutation <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \pi }">

  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H}"></span> equals <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle G}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>G</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle G}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f5f3c8921a3b352de45446a6789b104458c9f90b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.827ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle G}"></span>, and writes <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H=G}">

    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H=G}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d695258de13213a2c8c4a63706e2d0a74ef27f5e" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.989ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle H=G}"></span>.  Note that, with this definition of equality, graphs are self-dual:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msup>
          <mrow>
            <mo>(</mo>
            <msup>
              <mi>H</mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mo><!--  --></mo>
              </mrow>
            </msup>
            <mo>)</mo>
          </mrow>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mo><!--  --></mo>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>H</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/76a6a8a0368f71471235a8da2fb6fb562ac82f1b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.183ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \left(H^{*}\right)^{*}=H}"></span></dd></dl>
<p>A hypergraph <a href="/wiki/Automorphism" title="Automorphism">automorphism</a> is an isomorphism from a vertex set into itself, that is a relabeling of vertices. The set of automorphisms of a hypergraph <i>H</i> (= (<i>X</i>,&nbsp;<i>E</i>)) is a <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">group</a> under composition, called the <a href="/wiki/Automorphism_group" title="Automorphism group">automorphism group</a> of the hypergraph and written Aut(<i>H</i>).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Examples">Examples</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hypergraph&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Examples"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<p>In this example, <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle H}">
